    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4c46dd02b5d0403886d3b5340ec8fb25">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd7b83910-baa6-487b-91b5-81ebb8d26244'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d6641869" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Although the electrochemical properties of various cathode candidates for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are intensively studied on the material level, the evaluation and comparison from the perspective of future practical implementation are still lacking. In particular, air stability, which is often overlooked, will not only affect the electrode manufacturing processes but also cause detrimental structural change. Herein, two recently commercialized cathode products of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) are adopted to evaluate their stability in ambient atmospheres and investigate the structural degradation mechanisms. Theoretical calculations and experimental results demonstrate that the stability of NFM is significantly inferior to that of NVP during air exposure owing to the thermodynamically preferred chemical transformation of NFM. Furthermore, Raman mapping and X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements reveal the generation of alkaline, which accelerates the structural degradation of NFM. Accordingly, a simple second-sintering strategy is proposed to enhance the air stability of NFM by removing impurities and retrieving surface-precipitated Na<sup>+</sup> into the crystalline host. In situ XRD reveals the stabilized crystal structure after the second-sintering treatment not only effectively inhibits the chemical transformation of NFM in air but also endows the improved cycling stability (a high capacity retention of 90.7% after 200 cycles).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202308257-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202308257-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Large-scale energy storage systems have attracted tremendous interest in recent years because they are indispensable to regulating the electricity generated by renewable sources for peak load shifting owing to the intermittent features.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0001_R_d6641851e355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Differing from the conventional application scenarios of portable electronic devices and electric vehicles dominated by lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), lifetime operational cost, not volumetric, and gravimetric energy density, is the overriding factor for large-scale applications. In this regard, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are gaining attention due to the abundance and cost-effectiveness of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0002_R_d6641851e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0003_R_d6641851e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Reviewing the successful experience of LIBs, the adoption of appropriate technical routes and electrode materials should be pivotal on the cusp of the commercialization of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0004_R_d6641851e365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>On the anode side, hard carbon is unquestionably the most feasible option at the current stage,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0005_R_d6641851e373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which delivers a considerable Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity of 300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> without Na plating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0006_R_d6641851e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cathode materials have been mainly centered around layer-structured transition metal oxides, polyanions, and Prussian blue analogs , and there is no distinct tendency on which category should be the main objective for the successful commercialization of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0007_R_d6641851e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0008_R_d6641851e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the electrochemical properties of these cathode candidates (such as reversible capacity, cycling stability, and rate performance, etc.) have been intensively studied on the material level, the evaluation and comparison of different cathode materials from the perspective of future practical implementation and commercialization is still lacking.</p>
         
         <p>In actual industry production, air stability becomes a key factor to be considered as the dry or inert atmosphere protection is required during manufacture, transportation, and storage, resulting in a significant increase in cost. When exposed to air, Na<sup>+</sup> will extract from the bulk structure while the structure changes and the active Na species accumulate at the surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0009_R_d6641851e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Lett.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These active species will adsorb and react with air species (H<sub>2</sub>O and CO<sub>2</sub>) to form alkaline impurities, leading to an increase in impedance and a decrease in capacity of SIB. Various researches have been carried out to investigate the degradation of layered material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0010_R_d6641851e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a>-<a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0013_R_d6641851e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Sci. China Chem.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the reasons for the distinction in air stability of cathode material with various compositions and structures have not been clarified.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, the commercially available cathode materials NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) are selected to evaluate their storage performance, and the main reasons leading to the air sensitivity of NFM are revealed. Through comparison, NVP has superior storage performance than NFM, which owes to two key reasons (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0001">1a</a>). The residual alkali at the surface of NFM accelerates the accumulation of impurities and the structural degradation of NFM while the coated carbon layer of NVP (carbon content as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) prevents the further attack of air. Furthermore, the Gibbs free energy changes (Δ<i>G</i>) of the chemical transformation of cathode material with air species for NFM and NVP are remarkably different, and the chemical transformation of NFM is more thermodynamically favorable. Besides, a simple heating strategy is proposed to effectively enhance the storage performance of layered cathode material by removing surface impurities and strengthening structural stability. After exposing to harsh conditions for 7 days, the application of heating treatment can restore 84% capacity of aged NFM to the fresh material. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) results certify that the structural stability is strengthened through heating treatment and the chemical transformation of NFM with air species is thermodynamically inhibited. Consequently, the discharge capacity of heated NFM increased to 176% of the untreated sample at the same severe storing condition. Considering that the air sensitivity and additional treatment of NFM will increase the cost in electrode processing, the differences between NFM and NVP materials in terms of energy density, cyclability, cost of material, cost of dry room operation, and thermal stability are compared, thus concretizing the consideration factors. Accordingly, from the view of air stability, this work provides more information for the practical application of NVP and NFM materials, meanwhile, a targeted facile remediation strategy might be helpful to the wider application of air-sensitive materials like NFM, particularly.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308257-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/324e364f-2592-47c1-ae28-2c37f233843c/adfm202308257-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/324e364f-2592-47c1-ae28-2c37f233843c/adfm202308257-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e97c372c-4dd2-41d8-ac76-d6eefb2102e5/adfm202308257-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/324e364f-2592-47c1-ae28-2c37f233843c/adfm202308257-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202308257-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202308257-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The NFM and NVP materials are commercial materials and their composition and morphologies are characterized by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy and scanning electron microscopy (SEM) as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>;Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). NFM materials are uniform single crystalline particles with an average size of 8&nbsp;µm while NVP materials are spherical particles in the range of 8–18&nbsp;µm. The energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) mapping displays that various elements spread homogeneously in two samples, respectively. The XRD results confirm that all Bragg peaks of NFM material can be indexed to hexagonal O3─NaFeO<sub>2</sub> structure with R<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f07685d0-fc63-4677-97d0-5b84769f7abc/adfm202308257-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202308257:adfm202308257-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202308257-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>m space group (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), and the patterns of NVP material match the rhombohedral structure with the space group of R3c (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0014_R_d6641851e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Electrochem.&nbsp;commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To distinguish the storage properties of NFM and NVP materials, an aging experiment is fulfilled by exposing materials to moist air at 55&nbsp;°C for several days. After exposure, the weight of NFM and NVP samples is increased. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0001">1b</a>, the weight gain rate (WGR) of NFM (9.28%) is markedly higher than NVP (1.41%) after only one day of storage, indicating the poor air stability of NFM. The structural changes of NFM and NVP are compared by XRD measurement, referring to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0001">1c,d</a>. During the first 7 days of storage, all the XRD patterns of NVP are nearly consistent with the pristine samples, and the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0015_R_d6641851e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> do not appear until the NVP samples are exposed for 14 days (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). However, the NFM displays conspicuous air sensitivity. The (003) diffraction peak becoming broader and weaker suggests the phase transition of layered structure and the peaks of impurities can be easily observed just being exposed for 1&nbsp;day. Correspondingly, the differential scanning calorimetry-thermogravimetry (DSC–TG) curves establish huge mass loss of exposed NFM material (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), which is related to the extraction of H<sub>2</sub>O and deposition of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0016_R_d6641851e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The phase transformations of two materials during exposure mainly originate from the extraction of Na and the formation of sodium compounds on the surface, while the chemical reactions occur as follows:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0016_R_d6641851e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0017_R_d6641851e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202308257-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7c31164d-de5e-472b-b206-9aa76786ce82/adfm202308257-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202308257:adfm202308257-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202308257-math-0002.png">

                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi>
                                    <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>

                                       <mi>Na</mi>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msub>
                                    <msub>

                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mtd>
                           </mtr>
                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}&amp;&amp;{\mathrm{NaNi}}_{1/3}{\mathrm{Fe}}_{1/3}{\mathrm{Mn}}_{1/3}{{\mathrm{O}}}_2 + 1/2{\mathrm{x}}\;{{\mathrm{H}}}_2{\mathrm{O}} + 1/2{\mathrm{x}}\;{\mathrm{CO}}_2\nonumber\\ &amp;&amp; \to {\mathrm{Na}}_{1 - {\mathrm{x}}}{{\mathrm{H}}}_{\mathrm{x}}{\mathrm{Ni}}_{1/3}{\mathrm{Fe}}_{1/3}{\mathrm{Mn}}_{1/3}{{\mathrm{O}}}_2 + 1/2{\mathrm{x}}\;{\mathrm{Na}}_2{\mathrm{CO}}_3\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\rm{N}}{{\rm{a}}}_{\rm{3}}{{\rm{V}}}_2{\left( {{\rm{P}}{{\rm{O}}}_{\rm{4}}} \right)}_3 + {\rm{x }}{{\rm{H}}}_{\rm{2}}{\rm{O}} \to {\rm{N}}{{\rm{a}}}_{{\rm{3}} - {\rm{x}}}{{\rm{H}}}_{\rm{x}}{{\rm{V}}}_{\rm{2}}{\left( {{\rm{P}}{{\rm{O}}}_{\rm{4}}} \right)}_3 + {\rm{x NaOH}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The reactions are verified by the inductively coupled plasma (ICP) test, which is performed on the stored materials removing the surface impurity (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, with the adsorption of H<sub>2</sub>O, the formation of alkaline impurities and the structural conversion of NFM material severely decline their electrochemical performance. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0001">1f</a>, there is a capacity loss of 80&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after moist air exposure for 1&nbsp;h, and the generation of the phase transition region and the coating of alkaline compounds lead to a tremendous increase in polarization (0.67&nbsp;V). Notably, NFM almost can not release capacity after exposing for 1 day and the slurry becomes gelated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) due to the influence of H<sub>2</sub>O and alkali. In contrast, the first charge/discharge curves of aged NVP are nearly the same as fresh NVP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0001">1e</a>). To gain insight into air sensitive reaction mechanisms of NFM and NVP materials, density function theory (DFT) calculation is carried out to investigate their reactivity with air species (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0001">1g</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) and the calculated structure models are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9–S13</a> (Supporting Information). The Δ<i>G</i> of the above four chemical reactions at 300 K are marked as Δ<i>G</i><sub>1</sub>-Δ<i>G</i><sub>4</sub>, respectively. Referring to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0001">1g</a>, whether CO<sub>2</sub> is present in the moist atmosphere or not, the Δ<i>G</i> of chemical transformations of exposed NFM are lower than that of NVP in the same ambient atmosphere (Δ<i>G</i><sub>1</sub>&lt;Δ<i>G</i><sub>2</sub>, Δ<i>G</i><sub>3</sub>&lt;Δ<i>G</i><sub>4</sub>), indicating the exchange barrier of Na and H in NFM is much smaller than NVP. Besides, in the presence of CO<sub>2</sub>, all the Δ<i>G</i> of NFM and NVP decrease, but only the Δ<i>G</i><sub>1</sub>&lt;0 in 300&nbsp;K, which means the reaction of air and NFM is easy to perform, while that of NVP needs more motivation. It verifies that the difference in air stability of NFM and NVP fundamentally originates from the inherent structural distinction, while CO<sub>2</sub> promotes the chemical conversion, thus the transition of NFM to Na<sub>1−x</sub>H<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is spontaneous in the air.</p>
         
         <p>To further reveal the air stability distinction between NFM and NVP, a comprehensive characterization utilizing SEM, Raman spectroscopy, and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is conducted. After 7 days of air exposure, there is no obvious change in the SEM image of NVP material (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2a</a>), while the lumpy impurities and gliding steps are formed on NFM particle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). EDS mapping images show that the impurities contain Na and O elements, while Ni, Fe, and Mn elements are absent (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Combining with the XRD patterns, it can be inferred that the main impurity is Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. In addition, the phenomenon of lattice gliding is always observed in analogous single-crystalline cathode materials under high cut-off voltage<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0018_R_d6641851e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Science">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and accumulates with cycling,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0019_R_d6641851e1040" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is related to the local stress caused by the concentration gradient of lithium ions in the lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0020_R_d6641851e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0021_R_d6641851e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It means the reactions of H<sub>2</sub>O and CO<sub>2</sub> in the air with NFM material consume the Na<sup>+</sup> in the bulk phase and the difference of sodium ion concentration in bulk and surface region of material causes the ununiform structural stress, which results in the generation of gliding steps. Furthermore, the variation of the relative amount of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface of NFM material with the aging time is evaluated by Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2c</a>). The previous work shows that the two intense bands at 470 and 580&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to <i>A</i><sub>1g</sub> and <i>E</i><sub>g</sub> modes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0022_R_d6641851e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which originate from the stretching and bending vibrations of metal–oxygen bonds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0023_R_d6641851e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Stor. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. In addition, the emergence of a new peak at ≈1080 cm<sup>−1</sup> after exposure is attributed to Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface of NFM material,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0024_R_d6641851e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0025_R_d6641851e1091" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the intensity increasing with exposure time implies the generation and accumulation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>. XPS measurement is performed to further analyze the surface species variations of NFM and NVP after storing for 7 days. The O 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2d</a>) for NFM samples have four major peaks, in which the M─O peak (529.6&nbsp;eV) is classified as bulk lattice oxygen,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0026_R_d6641851e1104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nano Res.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the C═O peak (531.2&nbsp;eV), C─O peak (532.8&nbsp;eV) and O─H peak (535.5&nbsp;eV) are almost contributed from impurities such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and adsorbed OH<sup>−</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0027_R_d6641851e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Stor. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> separately. From the O1s spectra, the peak intensities of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> increase after 7 days exposure, indicating the formation of impurities during the aging process. However, after depth sputtering for 60&nbsp;s, the intensity of lattice oxygen is enhanced, meaning the formed impurities cover on the surface of NFM materials. The collected C 1s spectra contain C─C peak (284.8&nbsp;eV), C─O peak (286.2&nbsp;eV), and C═O peak (289.4&nbsp;eV) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2e</a>, and the results are consistent with O1s spectra. In addition, the peaks corresponding to Ni<sup>2+</sup> (855.4&nbsp;eV) and Ni<sup>3+</sup> (857.5&nbsp;eV)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0028_R_d6641851e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Carbon Neutralization">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are observed in Ni 2p spectra for NFM materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2f</a>). For fresh NFM sample, the main existence of Ni element is Ni<sup>2+</sup>, and a certain amount of Ni<sup>3+</sup> exists in NFM to balance the total charge due to the existence of Mn<sup>3+</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Besides, the content of Ni<sup>3+</sup> drops after 7 days, proving that the oxidation state of Ni changes as the impurities are generated and the structure of NFM collapses, which is consistent with the XRD results. Moreover, the C 1s, V 2p, and P 2p spectra of NVP are collected. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2g</a>, the C 1s spectra show three peaks of C─C, C─O, and C═O, which is the same with NFM. For P 2p spectra, a P═O doublet splitting peak<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0029_R_d6641851e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ChemSusChem">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> is observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2h</a>, which represents the existence of the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>. Besides, after storing for 7 days, there is no obvious change in the spectra, indicating the valence state of P in NVP is invariant. In addition, for pristine NVP, a couple of V splitting peaks located at 517.2 and 524.4&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0002">2i</a>) corresponds to the contributions of V 2p3/2 and V 2p1/2, which can be assigned to V<sup>3+</sup> valence state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0029_R_d6641851e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ChemSusChem">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0030_R_d6641851e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There is also no obvious difference in V 2p spectra after storing for 7 days, indicating the stability of the V element. It should be noted that a similar peak shift of V 2p spectra appears in the etching depth profiling of both the pristine NVP sample and the NVP-7-day sample, which means the carbon-coated layer affects the valence state of V during synthesis. Based on the above results, NVP material exhibits minimal changes during storing for 7 days and demonstrates better air stability than NFM. In conclusion, impurities generated on NFM visibly after air exposure and cause the structure to transform on the surface region of NFM material, while the NVP material exhibits minimal changes under the same condition. The accumulation of impurities not only induces the gelation of slurry, but also slows down the charge transfer kinetics at the interface between electrode and electrolyte with the increase in content of species with poor Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity. The insensitivity of NVP material to air and the suppression of carbonate formation on its surface result in superior electrochemical performance compared to NFM after exposure to air.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308257-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cdfc617f-81f7-4269-bbe5-ea216b518b76/adfm202308257-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cdfc617f-81f7-4269-bbe5-ea216b518b76/adfm202308257-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a1a9769d-a204-469a-a31c-269d8ada8046/adfm202308257-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cdfc617f-81f7-4269-bbe5-ea216b518b76/adfm202308257-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308257-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308257" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Before and after air exposure SEM images of a) NVP and b) NFM. c) Raman spectra of NFM during 7 days exposure. XPS spectra of d) O1s, e) C1s, f) Ni2p for NFM and g) C1s, h) V2p, i) P2p for NVP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to verify the distribution of impurities on the surface and within the bulk phase of NFM material, the 7 days-exposed samples are cut to expose cross sections for Raman imaging measurements. The linear scanning mode is performed along the green line from particle A to B as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0003">3a</a>. Notably, the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> only appears between NFM particles indicating the impurities can not invade into the bulk of material. At the same time, the peaks of metal–oxygen bonds widen with the increase of intensity for Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, suggesting the structure becomes disordered in this region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0025_R_d6641851e1219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It demonstrates the simultaneous occurrence of impurities formation and the structural transformation of NFM, and the phase change of NFM spreads from the surface to the bulk region overexposed time. Therefore, the structural characteristics of NFM gradually change over time in the XRD test. As the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, part of TM valence might get changed, such as the reduction of Ni<sup>3+</sup> and the release of O balance charges,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0009_R_d6641851e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Lett.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thus causing the formation of disordered regions near the surface, which is consistent with the XPS results. The disordered structure impedes the kinetics of Na diffusion in the solid phase. With the structural degradation of exposed NFM material, the kinetics of Na diffusion gradually decelerate,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0024_R_d6641851e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which results in remarkable polarization and capacity loss. Additionally, the cross-sectional SEM images and EDS mapping show that the block impurities containing Na and O elements while lacking Ni, Fe, and Mn elements generate in the middle of two particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0003">3b</a>), corresponding to alkaline species, which can further confirm the component and distribution of impurities.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308257-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/015792bf-f7a4-49d9-9a0d-606f5e699d17/adfm202308257-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/015792bf-f7a4-49d9-9a0d-606f5e699d17/adfm202308257-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4f66f543-18fb-4598-9086-6c41716f1ef0/adfm202308257-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/015792bf-f7a4-49d9-9a0d-606f5e699d17/adfm202308257-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308257-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308257" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cross-sectional optical images and Raman linear mapping test. b) Cross-sectional SEM image and corresponding EDS mappings of NFM sample after 7 days exposure.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In summary, it can be confirmed that when the NFM material is exposed to air, the Na<sup>+</sup> tends to extract from the lattice and the structural transformation occurs from surface to bulk, accompanying the impurities accumulation between NFM particles. The existence of disordered phase region and impurities reduce the capacity and electrochemical stability of NFM material markedly. Therefore, effectively improving the electrochemical performance of aged NFM materials requires reducing the content of disordered phase and impurities and promoting the reinsertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the NFM structure.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a simple heating strategy is carried out to remove the impurities. It has been confirmed that Na<sup>+</sup> can be inserted into the structure of the precursor at a low temperature of 500&nbsp;°C during the synthesis process of layered cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0031_R_d6641851e1273" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the adsorbed species and impurities begin desorbing and decomposing at a temperature lower than 650&nbsp;°C as TG curves shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). Consequently, when the heating temperature exceeds 650&nbsp;°C, the extracted Na<sup>+</sup> re-insert into the structural changed region to regain the orderliness of structure, which is verified by XRD results in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4a</a>. As mentioned above, the (003) and (104) peaks of NFM weaken and widen after 7 days air exposure. Encouragingly, after the heating treatment, the sharp peaks of (003) and (104) reappear. However, the peak of NiO impurity still exists, demonstrating the transformation of structure is not completely reversible. The electrochemical performance of stored NFM partly recovers after the heating treatment due to the reinsertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, which is consistent with XRD results. The discharge capacity of heated NFM (NFM-7day-heat) restores to 84% of fresh NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4b</a>), which has significant difference with exposed NFM material. Notably, the reinsertion of Na<sup>+</sup> plays a crucial role in the recovery of exposed NFM, thus the structure and capacity cannot recover if the extracted Na is removed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4b</a>, NFM-7day-wash-heat).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308257-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c145954b-aaf8-4cbb-b83e-333721f9774e/adfm202308257-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c145954b-aaf8-4cbb-b83e-333721f9774e/adfm202308257-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/97e27709-1f3e-4b75-b865-e2a4076a224e/adfm202308257-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c145954b-aaf8-4cbb-b83e-333721f9774e/adfm202308257-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308257-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308257" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of pristine, exposed, and heating-treated NFM materials. b) The first charge/discharge curves of samples. c) FT–IR spectra of pristine and heated NFM materials. d) XRD-patterns and e) first charge/discharge curves of heated NFM after exposure. e) Rate performance and g) cycling performance of pristine and heated NFM. In situ EIS profiles of h) NFM and i) heated NFM between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V at 0.1&nbsp;C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, this strategy is found to be similarly operative with fresh material. According to the ICP results of NFM in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the content of Na atom is higher than the stoichiometric ratio in NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, indicating a small amount of residual alkali generated on fresh NFM material during production inevitably. The hygroscopicity of residual alkali will promote the adsorption of H<sub>2</sub>O on the NFM surface, leading to accelerated extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus the degradation of the structure, and the formation of impurities speed up. From the Fourier transform infrared (FT–IR) spectra of fresh NFM, peaks corresponding to NaHCO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> impurities are observed as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4c</a>. After heating treatment, the peaks’ intensity of impurities become weakened or even disappear, indicating the decomposition of impurities. The heating treatment promotes the decomposition of residual alkali and the reinsertion of Na into the bulk of NFM, consequently, the structural deterioration of NFM is suppressed. The conclusion is confirmed through ICP and XRD measurement as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4d</a>. After removing the surface impurity by washing with a small amount of H<sub>2</sub>O and ethanol, and the Na content of bulk of heated NFM is higher than that of NFM (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, after 1 day of exposure, the peaks corresponding to O3 layered structure maintain sharp in heated NFM and the peaks of impurities can hardly be observed. After 1&nbsp;h of exposure, the heated NFM delivers a discharge capacity of 61.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, increasing to 176% of fresh NFM (35.1&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with the same air exposure condition, referring to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4e</a>. Obviously, the air stability of NFM is improved and the generation of alkaline impurities on the surface of NFM material is suppressed after heating treatment. Additionally, due to the reinsertion of Na and the decrease of impurities, the discharge capacity of heated NFM (126.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) is higher than fresh NFM (122.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 0.1&nbsp;C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, the rate performance and cycling stability are enhanced as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4f,g</a>. The difference in Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics of fresh NFM and heated NFM at the cathode/electrolyte interface is investigated through in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4h,i</a>). The surface film resistance of heated NFM decreases, demonstrating the reduced barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> and electronic migration, which is consistent with the difference in rate performance. Referring to the above results, heating treatment can effectively remove the residual alkali and insert Na into the bulk structure, resulting in higher discharge capacity, faster Na<sup>+</sup> migration, and suppression of structural gradation, thus the storage performance of NFM material is effectively improved.</p>
         
         <p>To further explore the detailed structure evolution of NFM and heated NFM during sodiation and desodiation, in situ XRD is carried out during the first charge/discharge cycle (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0005">5</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). For NFM sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0005">5a</a>), upon charging, the (003) and (006) peaks shift to a lower angle while the (101), (012), and (104) peaks shift to a higher angle, which indicates a solid–solution reaction of O3 structure with expansion of c-axis and contraction of lattice parameter a.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0032_R_d6641851e1406" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Stor. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As subsequent Na<sup>+</sup> extraction, the (003) and (006) peaks split in two respectively and the new peaks are observed at ≈15.6&nbsp;° and 31.6&nbsp;°, accompanied by the obvious reduction of (104) peak intensity and the formation of (015) peak, which corresponds to a biphasic reaction of O3─P3 at ≈3.0&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0033_R_d6641851e1413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Stor. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the (104) peak disappears, the structure transforms to P3 phase completely, and a monophasic solid–solution reaction of P3 occurs with the continuous shift of peaks assigned to P3 phase till charge to 4.0&nbsp;V. During the reverse discharging process, the structure experiences a completely opposite evolution of P3–O3, indicating the structure of NFM is basically reversible during sodiation and desodiation. It is worth noting that the structural reversibility of heated NFM is superior to NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0005">5b,c</a>), as the heated NFM has a smaller angle shift (0.021&nbsp;°) of (003) peak compared with NFM (0.101&nbsp;°) after discharge. When charged to 4.0&nbsp;V, the lattice parameter <i>c</i> variation is 1.26% for NFM and 0.09% for heated NFM indicating a larger volume change of NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0005">5d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The huge volume change results in higher stress and larger irreversible damage to layered structure, thus the structural stability decreases, which is corresponding to the poorer cycle performance of NFM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0004">4g</a>). Heating treatment can promote the reinsertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the alkaline impurities into the bulk NFM, thus stabilizing the crystal structure, alleviating the structural stress, and enhancing the structural reversibility. Heated NFM exhibits greater structural stability thus the adverse chemical transformation of heated NFM in air is thermodynamically suppressed, which is an important reason for its improved air stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308257-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/10abb0ff-1c4f-4461-8ac5-43e2a2c87b16/adfm202308257-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/10abb0ff-1c4f-4461-8ac5-43e2a2c87b16/adfm202308257-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8e993dad-da9d-42c4-b173-c3955cc6b565/adfm202308257-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/10abb0ff-1c4f-4461-8ac5-43e2a2c87b16/adfm202308257-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308257-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308257" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Contour map of in situ XRD patterns for a,c) NFM and heated NFM between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V during the first charge/discharge cycles. b) Contour map of in situ XRD patterns between 14.5° and 17°. d) The change of lattice parameter c during the first charge/discharge cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The comparison of energy density, thermal stability, cyclability, cost of material, and cost of electrode manufacturing for NFM and NVP is shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202308257-fig-0006">6</a>. The energy density of the material is based on its practical achievable capacity and average voltage. NVP displays high cycling performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c</a>, Supporting Information), but the existence of vanadium raises the cost of material compared with iron-based polyanionic cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0034_R_d6641851e1472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NFM exhibits higher reversible capacity, energy density, and superior thermal stability. With the existence of electrolyte, the exothermic peaks of NVP and NFM appear at lower temperature (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a>, Supporting Information) compared with dry materials (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a>, Supporting Information). The drop in exothermic temperature of NVP is more obvious than NFM, which means the catalytic decomposition of electrolyte by transition metal elements of NVP is more serious.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0035_R_d6641851e1484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J Energy Chem.">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0036_R_d6641851e1484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Energy Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the cost of NFM material is close to NVP due to the use of costly nickel compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0037_R_d6641851e1489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Acc. Chem. Res.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the air sensitivity and additional treatment of NFM will increase the cost of electrode manufacturing. The manufacture of NFM electrode requires to be carried out in a larger dry room with lower moisture content, consequently the cost of electricity and the operation cost of dry room increase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0038_R_d6641851e1495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Power Sources">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In general, NVP and NFM have advantages in different directions, and they are suitable for various application scenarios.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202308257-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/74ddae8d-0d8e-46ab-bfad-867b4777a820/adfm202308257-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/74ddae8d-0d8e-46ab-bfad-867b4777a820/adfm202308257-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3c75b4f2-0d60-496a-852b-4d2241a4b243/adfm202308257-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/74ddae8d-0d8e-46ab-bfad-867b4777a820/adfm202308257-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202308257-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202308257" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Radar plots of NFM and NVP across five key categories: energy density, thermal stability, cyclability, cost of material, and cost of dry room operation.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0034" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0034_R_d6641851e1520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0037" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0037_R_d6641851e1520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Acc. Chem. Res.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202308257-bib-0038" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202308257-bib-0038_R_d6641851e1520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J.                </figcaption>

         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202308257-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202308257-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the air stability of two commercial sodium cathode materials NFM and NVP is estimated systematically, which displays a remarkable difference in air sensitivity. The distinction primarily originates from their inherent structure stability of reacting with air components. DFT calculations show the Gibbs free energy change of NFM reacting with air is much lower than NVP, suggesting the chemical transformation to generate impurities at the surface of NFM is thermodynamically favorable. Moreover, the inevitable residual alkali promotes the structural degradation of NFM, thus inhibiting the Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics and the discharge capacity. Correspondingly, a heating treatment strategy is performed to recover or boost the electrochemical performance and suppress the deterioration of NFM before or after storing. As a result, the discharge capacity of heated NFM increase to 176% compared with fresh NFM, which are both exposed to a severe condition. The practicability and effectiveness of heating strategy can make it widely applicable for air-sensitive layered oxides cathode material. Furthermore, the differences of NFM and NVP across five categories are compared. NFM and NVP exhibit superior performance in different dimensions separately, which provides a new perspective to evaluate different cathode materials for future practical implementation and commercialization.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
